//<![CDATA[



/* JQUERY */
jQuery(document).ready(function(){ 
    
   
  

	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish(); */
	/* jQuery('ul#rmenu').superfish().find('ul').bgIframe({opacity:false}); */
 
	/* For IE6 */
	if (jQuery.browser.msie && /MSIE 6\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 7\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent) && !/MSIE 8\.0/i.test(window.navigator.userAgent)) {

		/* Max-width for images in IE6 */		
		var centerwidth = jQuery("td#middle").width(); 
		
		/* Images without caption */
		jQuery(".post img").each(function() { 
			var maxwidth = centerwidth - 10 + 'px';
			var imgwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var imgheight = jQuery(this).height(); 
			var newimgheight = (centerwidth / imgwidth * imgheight) + 'px';	
			if (imgwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxwidth}); 
				jQuery(this).css({height: newimgheight}); 
			}
		});
		
		/* Images with caption */
		jQuery("div.wp-caption").each(function() { 
			var captionwidth = jQuery(this).width(); 
			var maxcaptionwidth = centerwidth + 'px';
			var captionheight = jQuery(this).height();
			var captionimgwidth =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").width();
			var captionimgheight =  jQuery("div.wp-caption img").height();
			if (captionwidth > centerwidth) { 
				jQuery(this).css({width: maxcaptionwidth}); 
				var newcaptionheight = (centerwidth / captionwidth * captionheight) + 'px';
				var newcaptionimgheight = (centerwidth / captionimgwidth * captionimgheight) + 'px';
				jQuery(this).css({height: newcaptionheight}); 
				jQuery("div.wp-caption img").css({height: newcaptionimgheight}); 
				}
		});
		
		/* sfhover for LI:HOVER support in IE6: */
		jQuery("ul li").
			hover( function() {
					jQuery(this).addClass("sfhover")
				}, 
				function() {
					jQuery(this).removeClass("sfhover")
				} 
			); 

	/* End IE6 */
	}
	
	
	
	/* Since 3.7.8: Auto resize videos (embed and iframe elements) 
	TODO: Parse parent's dimensions only once per layout column, not per video
	*/
	function bfa_resize_video() {
		jQuery('embed, iframe').each( function() {
			var video = jQuery(this),
			videoWidth = video.attr('width'); // use the attr here, not width() or css()
			videoParent = video.parent(),
			videoParentWidth = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'width' ) ),
			videoParentBorder = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-left-width' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'border-right-width' ) ),
			videoParentPadding = parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-left' ) ) 
										+  parseFloat( videoParent.css( 'padding-right' ) ),
			maxWidth = videoParentWidth - videoParentBorder - videoParentPadding;

			if( videoWidth > maxWidth ) {
				var videoHeight = video.attr('height'),
				videoMaxHeight = ( maxWidth / videoWidth * videoHeight );
				video.attr({ width: maxWidth, height: videoMaxHeight });
			} 

		});	
	}
	bfa_resize_video();
	jQuery(window).resize( bfa_resize_video );

		
	jQuery(".post table tr").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("over");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("over");
		});

	
	jQuery(".post table tr:even").
		addClass("alt");

	
	jQuery("input.text, input.TextField, input.file, input.password, textarea").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("highlight"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("highlight"); 
		})
	
	jQuery("input.inputblur").
		focus(function () {  
			jQuery(this).addClass("inputfocus"); 
		}).
		blur(function () { 
			jQuery(this).removeClass("inputfocus"); 
		})

		

	
	jQuery("input.button, input.Button, input#submit").
		mouseover(function() {
			jQuery(this).addClass("buttonhover");
		}).
		mouseout(function() {
			jQuery(this).removeClass("buttonhover");
		});

	/* toggle "you can use these xhtml tags" */
	jQuery("a.xhtmltags").
		click(function(){ 
			jQuery("div.xhtml-tags").slideToggle(300); 
		});

	/* For the Tabbed Widgets plugin: */
	jQuery("ul.tw-nav-list").
		addClass("clearfix");

		
	
});

//]]>

								<div class="post-972 post type-post status-publish format-standard hentry category-cpp-tutorial odd" id="post-972">
						<div class="post-headline">		<h1>1.4a &#8212; A first look at function parameters</h1>
		</div>		<div class="post-byline">By Alex, on January 25th, 2015</div>		<div class="post-bodycopy clearfix"><p><strong>Parameters</strong></p>
<p>In the return values subsection, you learned that a function can return a value back to the caller.  <strong>Parameters</strong> are used to allow the caller to pass information into a function!  This allows functions to be written to perform generic tasks without having to worry about the specific values used, and leaves the exact values of the variables up to the caller.  </p>
<p>This is a case that is best learned by example.  Here is an example of a very simple function that adds two numbers together and returns the result to the caller.</p>
<p></p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-1">1</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-2">2</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-3">3</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-4">4</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-5">5</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-6">6</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-7">7</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-8">8</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-9">9</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-10">10</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-11">11</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-12">12</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-13">13</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-14">14</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-15">15</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-16">16</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-17">17</div></div>

class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-1"><span class="crayon-c">//#include &lt;stdafx.h&gt; // Visual Studio users need to uncomment this line</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-2"><span class="crayon-p">#include &lt;iostream&gt;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-3">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-4"><span class="crayon-c">// add takes two integers as parameters, and returns the result of their sum</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-5"><span class="crayon-c">// add does not care what the exact values of x and y are</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-6"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-7"><span class="crayon-sy">{</span></div><div </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">+</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">y</span><span id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-10">&nbsp;</div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-11"><span class="crayon-t">int</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">main</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-12"><span crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-14"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-c">// It is the caller of add() that decides the exact values of x and y</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-15"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">4</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">5</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span add()</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-16"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-st">return</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece435195469054-17"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0020 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>When function add() is called, x is assigned the value 4, and y is assigned the value 5.  The compiler evaluates x + y, which is the value 9, and then returns this value to the caller.  This value of 9 is then sent to cout (by main()) to be printed on the screen.</p>
<p>Output:</p>
<pre>
9
</pre>
<p>Note that since our add function takes two parameters, our function call to add() is now composed of the function name, plus a parameter list with two values.</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s take a look at a couple of other calls to functions():</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">1</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">multiply</span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">1</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-cn">0</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece445029254271-31"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0055 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>This program produces the output:</p>
<pre>
9
6
15
10
7
6
</pre>
<p>The first two statements are straightforward.</p>
<p>In the third statement, the parameters are expressions that get evaluated before being passed.  In this case, 1 + 2 evaluates to 3, so 3 is passed to x.  3 * 4 evaluates to 12, so 12 is passed to y.  add(3, 12) resolves to 15.</p>
<p>The next pair of statements is relatively easy as well:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

<p></p>
<p>In this case, add() is called where x = a and y = a.  Since a = 5, add(a, a) = add(5, 5), which resolves to 10.</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s take a look at the first tricky statement in the bunch:</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ece454319533931" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece454319533931-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece454319533931-1"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">1</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">multiply</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">2</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">3</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">endl</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-c">// evaluates 1 + (2 * 3)</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0008 seconds] -->
<p></p>
<p>When the function add() is executed, the CPU needs to determine what the values for parameters x and y are.  x is simple since we just passed it the integer 1, so it assigns x=1.  To get a value for y, it needs to evaluate multiply(2, 3) first.  The CPU assigns z = 2 and w = 3, and multiply(2, 3) returns the integer value 6.  That return value of 6 can now be assigned to the y parameter of the add() function.  add(1,6) returns the integer 7, which is then passed to cout for printing.</p>
<p>Put less verbosely (where the => symbol is used to represent evaluation):<br />
add(1, multiply(2, 3)) => add(1, 6) => 7</p>
<p>The following statement looks tricky because one of the parameters given to add() is another call to add().</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ece45b821913173" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ece45b821913173-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece45b821913173-1"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">1</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">add</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-cn">2</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">3</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">endl</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-c">// evalues 1 + (2 + 3)</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0007 seconds] -->
<p>But this case works exactly the same as the above case where one of the parameters is a call to multiply().</p>
<p>Before the CPU can evaluate the outer call to add(), it must evaluate the inner call to add(2, 3).  add(2, 3) evaluates to 5.  Now it can evaluate add(1, 5), which evaluates to the value 6.  cout is passed the value 6.</p>
<p>Less verbosely:<br />
add(1, add(2, 3)) => add(1, 5) => 6</p>
<p><strong>Effectively using functions</strong></p>
<p>One of the biggest challenges new programmers encounter (besides learning the language) is learning when and how to use functions effectively.  Functions offer a great way to break your program up into manageable and reusable parts, which can then be easily connected together to perform a larger and more complex task.  By breaking your program into smaller parts, the overall complexity of the program is reduced, which makes the program both easier to write and to modify.</p>
<p>Typically, when learning C++, you will write a lot of programs that involve 3 subtasks:</p>
<ol>
<li>Reading inputs from the user
<li>Calculating a value from the inputs
<li>Printing the calculated value
</ol>
<p>For simple programs, reading inputs from the user can generally be done in main().  However, step #2 is a great candidate for a function.  This function should take the user inputs as a parameter, and return the calculated value.  The calculated value can then be printed (either directly in main(), or by another function if the calculated value is complex or has special printing requirements).</p>
<p>A good rule of thumb is that each function should perform one (and only one) task.  New programmers often write functions that combine steps 2 and 3 together.  However, because calculating a value and printing it are two different tasks, this violates the one and only one task guideline.  Ideally, a function that calculates a value should return the value to the caller and let the caller decide what to do with the calculated value.</p>

<p></p>
<p>4) Write a function called doubleNumber() that takes one integer parameter and returns twice the value passed in.</p>
<p>5) Write a complete program that reads an integer from the user (using cin, discussed in section 1.3), doubles it using the doubleNumber() function you wrote for question 4, and then prints the doubled value out to the console.</p>
<p><strong>Quiz Answers</strong></p>
<p>To see these answers, select the area below with your mouse.</p>
<p>1) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id6397630'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id6397630" style="display:none">multiply() is defined as returning void, which means it can&#8217;t return a value.  Since the function is trying to return a value, this function will produce a compiler error.  The function should return an int.</div>
<p>2) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id1575409470'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id1575409470" style="display:none">multiply() calculates a value and puts the result in a variable, but never returns the value to the caller.  Because there is no return statement, and the function is supposed to return an int, this will produce a compiler error.</div>
<p>3) <a class="solution_link_show" href="javascript:void(0)" onclick="wpSolutionToggle(document.getElementById('id1182989376'), this, 'Show Solution', 'Hide Solution')">Show Solution</a></p>
<div class="solution_div" id="id1182989376" style="display:none">multiply is called where x = add(1, 2, 3), and y = 4.  First, the CPU resolves x = add(1, 2, 3), which returns 1 + 2 + 3, or x = 6.  multiply(6, 4) = 24, which is the answer.</div>

class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece47e835082752-23"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">=</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">doubleNumber</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line crayon-striped-line" id="crayon-555d747ece47e835082752-24"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">endl</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ece47e835082752-25"><span class="crayon-h">&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;</span><span id="crayon-555d747ece47e835082752-26"><span class="crayon-sy">}</span></div></div></td>
					</tr>
				</table>
			</div>
		</div>
<!-- [Format Time: 0.0032 seconds] -->

</div>
<p>Note: You may come up with other (similar) solutions for #4 and #5.  There are often many ways to do the same thing in C++.</p>

int main()   
{   
    using namespace std;

</pre>
<pre>int doubleNumber(int x){

int main(){
	using namespace std;

	cin >> x;
	cout < < doubleNumber(x) << endl;
	return 0;
}</PRE>

it works fine. So my question is: I thought the placement of &#8220;main()&#8221; within the code isn&#8217;t supposed to matter? 				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-6765">
		January 28, 2008 at 3:44 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=6765#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-6765", "6765", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Dan, when the compiler compiles your program, it reads through the files sequentially.  This means if it hasn&#8217;t encountered doubleNumber() by the time you try to use doubleNumber(), then it will give you an error.</p>
<p>There are two ways to fix this:<br />
1) Put your functions above main()<br />
2) Use a function prototype.  This means, put the function declaration <code>int doubleNumber(int x);</code> above main(), and leave the actual function definition below main().  That way, when the compiler encounters doubleNumber(), it will know what to expect, even if you haven&#8217;t actually defined how doubleNumber() is implemented yet.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">

		<p>When developing single-file programs, yes.  But when you get into multi-file programs, things become a bit more complex.  In the case of multi-file programs, the typical way of doing this is to put the function prototypes in a header file, and then #include the header file wherever you need access to those functions.  I cover this in more detail in <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/19-header-files/" rel="nofollow">section 1.9 -- header files</a></p>

<p>int doubleNumber(int x, int y)<br />
{<br />
	return x * y;<br />

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=32304#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-32304", "32304", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>It look like you put your Doublenumber() function right in the middle of your main() function.  Functions can&#8217;t be nested in C++.</p>
<p>Just move Doublenumber() above main() and you will be one step closer.</p>
<p>Also, when you call Doublenumber, it should be like this: Doublenumber(x)</p>

		<p>I&#8217;m confused by this:</p>
<pre>int add(int x, int y, int z)
{
    return x + y + z;
}

int multiply(int x, int y)
{
    return x * y;
}

int main()

{
    cout &lt;&lt; multiply(add(1, 2, 3), 4) &lt;&lt; endl;
    return 0;
}</pre>
<p>Can you explain how x, y and z are being attributed there. I thought you had to say x = 7 or whatever, but here there&#8217;s nothing like that. Is it down to the order you write x, y and z in along with the order of the numbers later on? Or will it just attribute the values to x first, then y, then z, no matter what order you wrote them in (e.g. z, x, y)?</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-58883">
		<div id="div-comment-58883" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/10dc9f8052d5a4b080c620a4467e54f7?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/10dc9f8052d5a4b080c620a4467e54f7?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">csvan</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-58883">
		April 23, 2009 at 4:51 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=58883#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-58883", "58883", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to csvan'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>When you call the function add(int x, int y, int z), the compiler is expecting 3 integer values within the parentheses, in that exact order, separated by commas. The integer values that are passed this way are in turn assigned to x, y, and z. </p>
<p>Thus, when you write this call: add(1,2,3), it is equivalent to add(x = 1, y  = 2, z = 3), and the functions will treat these variables (x y and z) as having those values, as long as the function is running. </p>
<p>In other words, by calling add(1,2,3), you are really giving values to the variables in question, just as it should be.</p>
<p>It helps if you try to think of functions as separate from each other, just like Alex explained: A function returns a value to the caller, and the caller can pass parameters to the function being called. What this means effectively is that x, y and z are variables LOCAL to the function add (not main()!), and main simply helps the function add by telling it what value it should give to its variables.</p>
<p>Hope that helps! Send a mail otherwise (csvanefalk@hushmail.me). Of course, you should also ask Alex, I don&#8217;t know how often I will be checking these comments!</p>
		</div>

		<p>I may be wrong on this, but I think this is because of &#8220;rollover.&#8221; in other words, there is a maximum value that can be stored as an integer, and as I read somewhere else, when you perform an operation on a positive integer that makes it larger than the max, it rolls the number over to the smallest number (kinda like an odometer). In the case of integers, its a negative number.<br />
I first learned MATLAB programming, and when you went overboard there, it just returned &#8220;inf&#8221; for infinity. At least with that I could understand what had happened lol.</p>

		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-95007">
		<div id="div-comment-95007" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/9b579e4a841a0b61e388bb63a1294f05?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/9b579e4a841a0b61e388bb63a1294f05?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">madryoch</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-95007">
		December 6, 2010 at 10:50 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=95007#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95007", "95007", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to madryoch'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Hmm i hope this helps. When you type int variableName; it creates a reference in RAM for variableName and occupies a specific number of bytes for int types.Either you initialize the variable or not, the number of bytes occupied by the variable are specific for ALL variables of a specific type.Now There are types of int &#8230; e.g. unsigned short int and signed short int both take same bytes in RAM which means they can hold up to a specific maximum of different numbers.<br />
Unsigned short int range = numbers from -32768 to 32767. Total numbers stored = 32767-(-32768)= 65536 numbers<br />
Signed short int range = numbers from 0 to 65535. Total numbers stored = 65535 plus one which would be the number zero = 65536 numbers total&#8230;</p>
<p>So when u type int the compiler automatically sets it to signed int&#8230;<br />
What happens then when u make mathematical equations that exceed the limits ?<br />
The extra digits of the calculations are overflowing so they never get saved&#8230; which results in the following for signed short int&#8230;</p>
<p>Let&#8217;s say we have a result of 32768&#8230;= 32767+1 so it restarts at -32768.<br />
Further Explanation with binary system&#8230;</p>
<p>number 15 = 1&#215;8 1&#215;4 1&#215;2 1&#215;1 = 1111 in binary digits<br />
number 16 = 1&#215;16 0x8 0x4 0x2 0x1 =10000 in binary digits </p>
<p>Imagine now that u can only keep 4 binary digits(ammount of memmory space a variable of specific type takes) &#8230;<br />
So 10000 that has 5 digits looses the first digit and is saved as 0000. Number 0 in decimal<br />
4 binary digits means numbers from 0 to 15 (16 total numbers) That way if u try to store a larger number the counter will start anew as explained above&#8230;</p>
<p>Sorry if my explanation is not very good . English is not my native language but i really hope u understand how it goes&#8230;</p>
		</div>

		<p>Why doesn&#8217;t this work?</p>
<p></p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ed3f86114470289" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">

<p></p>
<p>Because you&#8217;ve defined your function to take two parameters, and if you want to double z, you need to pass in 2 as the second parameter.</p>
<p>That said, it&#8217;s weird to call your function doubleNumber when it actually multiplies two numbers (one of which may or may not be 2).</p>

<p></p>
<p>The problem is that the value is not doubling. Doing a debug, I saw that the function was called correctly, x was calculated as it should inside the function, but never returned to main. Doing a x=myFunction(x) resolves it, but I don&#8217;t understand why? If I&#8217;m simply using a myFunction(x) isn&#8217;t x supposed to get the new value calculated inside the function?</p>

		December 7, 2010 at 4:09 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=95011#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95011", "95011", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to madryoch'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Try using x=doubleNumber(x); instead of doubleNumber(x);</p>
<p>or </p>
<p>Remove doubleNumber(x); and correct the following to:</p>
<p>cout &lt;&lt; &quot;The result is &quot; &lt;&lt; doubleNumber(x);</p>
<p>The reason this happens would be if i am not mistaken that x variable in doubleNumber function is active only while in the function itself so when the control returns to main the x in main being a different variable has the set value of what u ve inserted with cin.<br />
Note that the returned value of ur doubleNumber function is not saved anywhere thus is not accessible&#8230; </p>
<p>So the solution is either assign the returned value to x in main function or ask from cout to execute the doubleNumber function and print the returned value right away&#8230;</p>
<p>Hope i helped&#8230; <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>

<p>it gives me this error:<br />
In function &#8220;int main()<br />
&#8220;multiply&#8221; undeclared (first use this function)</p>

		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-82688">
		<div id="div-comment-82688" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/135ab0961822790f7bc190efebde7f30?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/135ab0961822790f7bc190efebde7f30?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Howard</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-82688">
		April 10, 2010 at 8:48 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=82688#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-82688", "82688", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Howard'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Marco, you misspelled multiply when you wrote the function.  But you spelled it correctly when you called it from main. So the compiler was expecting a function called multiply, which doesn&#8217;t exist in your program, although one called multipliy does. To fix the error, either change the function name to the correct spelling, or misspell it the same way in the function call. I&#8217;d go for my first suggestion, though either will work.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

<p>This,</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ed393c751647355" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

<p></p>
<p>I understand that this is a small simple program where performance isn&#8217;t an issue, but if hypothetically this was a program with a much larger scale, is there a difference in speed/performance between these two techniques?  The first one seems shorter, and therefore faster?</p>
		</div>

		<p>The program with x = doubleNumber(x) has an extra assignment operation that happens, so it would be slightly slower.</p>
<p>The speed difference would likely be unnoticible unless you were running this thousands of times.</p>

		<p>Here is what I came up with for question 5</p>
<p></p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ed3590200192343" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">

<p>It works, but is not included in the answers&#8230;so, is it a correct way?</p>

		<p>If you define &#8220;correct&#8221; as producing the right answer, then it looks correct.  There are usually many ways to do the same thing in C++.  Sometimes one way is better than another, but other times, it&#8217;s just a matter of preference.</p>
<p>You could improve this program by getting rid of line 12 (int y=2) and changing the last line to:</p>
<p></p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ed26a6029408346" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed26a6029408346-1">1</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div class="crayon-line" id="crayon-555d747ed26a6029408346-1"><span class="crayon-r">cout</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-s">"Number entered multiplied by 2= "</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-e">multiply</span><span class="crayon-sy">(</span><span class="crayon-v">x</span><span class="crayon-sy">,</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-cn">2</span><span class="crayon-sy">)</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-o">&lt;&lt;</span><span class="crayon-h"> </span><span class="crayon-v">endl</span><span class="crayon-sy">;</span></div></div></td>

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=95374#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95374", "95374", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to cooper507'> &middot; Reply</a></span>

		<p>in the piece of code, for example:</p>
<p>int add(int x, int y)<br />
{<br />
    return x + y;<br />
}</p>
<p>does it matter that when you create a function you have the (int x, int y) in the parenthesis? what does it do for the function? </p>
<p>heres another example:</p>
<p>int multiply(int z, int w)<br />
{<br />
    return z * w;<br />
}</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251114">
		<div id="div-comment-251114" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-251114">
		January 9, 2015 at 5:07 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=251114#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251114", "251114", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>(int x, int y) are the function parameters.  Without them, the compiler won&#8217;t know what x and y are inside of the add() function, and you won&#8217;t be able to pass values into add().</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->
</ul><!-- .children -->

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=95542#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95542", "95542", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to ingthinks'> &middot; Reply</a></span>

		<p>Here is my solution to #5:</p>
<p>#include &#8220;stdafx.h&#8221;<br />
#include </p>
<p>int doubleNumber(int x) // doubles value of x<br />
{<br />
	return x * 2;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
	using namespace std;<br />
	int y;              // declares variable y<br />
	int x;              // declares variable x<br />
	cout &lt;&gt; x;           // defines x as users input<br />
	y = x;              // defines y as equal to x to display the number that was doubled<br />
	cout &lt;&lt; y &lt;&lt; &quot; doubled is &quot; &lt;&lt; doubleNumber(x) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
	return 0;<br />
}</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-95543">
		<div id="div-comment-95543" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/21b2deaaa0bcf172aa88051b6cf7ba1f?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/21b2deaaa0bcf172aa88051b6cf7ba1f?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://127.0.0.1/' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>zingmars</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-95543">
		June 9, 2011 at 11:03 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=95543#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-95543", "95543", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to zingmars'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>You don&#8217;t actually need y, as you can simply do this<br />
cout &lt;&lt; x &lt;&lt; &quot; doubled is &quot; &lt;&lt; doubleNumber(x) &lt;&lt; When you take the x the first time, it will hold the original number, when you use doubleNumber, it will output the result of x * 2.<br />
That way the code is more optimized, as it requires less memory to hold the values, and it will no longer need to assign the value of x to y, which is a waste of CPU cycles (I know that in this case the difference will be a nanosecond at most, however in bigger programs this difference might be seconds).</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">

<p>#include(less than)iostream(more than)<br />
#include(less than)conio.h(more than)<br />
using namespace std;<br />
int main(){//returns 0<br />
    double dA,dB,dC;<br />
    cout&lt;&gt;dA;<br />
    cout&lt;&gt;dB;<br />
    dC=dA+dB;<br />
    cout&lt;&lt;dA&lt;&lt;&quot; + &quot;&lt;&lt;dB&lt;&lt;&quot; = &quot;&lt;&lt;dC&lt;&gt;dA;<br />
    cin&gt;&gt;dB;<br />
    dC=dA+dB;<br />
    cout&lt;&lt;dC;<br />

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=96478#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-96478", "96478", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Grif'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Very much a beginner here, but there&#8217;s something I need a little clarification with - switching the order of &#8220;main()&#8221; and &#8220;add(x, y)&#8221; in the code seems to cause an error (I was trying to input it that way due to my own OCD wanting main to be up the top of the code). </p>
<p>The result seemed to be that &#8220;main()&#8221; had no idea that &#8220;add(x, y)&#8221; even existed unless &#8220;add(x, y)&#8221; was assigned/created in code *before* &#8220;main()&#8221;.</p>
<p>Is this always the case? That &#8220;function a&#8221; trying to call &#8220;function b&#8221; requires function b to be defined (and placed literally above function a&#8217;s code in the editor) before it&#8217;s run?</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-96721">
		<div id="div-comment-96721" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/024dece87a61b55a9d728c8836935435?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/024dece87a61b55a9d728c8836935435?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">sjm71</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-96721">
		December 22, 2011 at 3:38 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=96721#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-96721", "96721", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to sjm71'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>Yes, this is always the case. You can also use function prototypes before main() instead of writing the full funtion definitions and the write the funtion definitions after main()</p>
<p>#include </p>
<p>//Function prototypes<br />
int add(int, int); // NOTE: the semicolon (;) at the end!</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
<br />
return 0;<br />
}</p>
<p>int add(int x, int y)<br />
{<br />
return x + y;<br />
}</p>
<p>Hope this helps?</p>
		</div>

		<p>does the doprint function have to be typed before the main function?<br />
Also, does doprint have to be capitalized?</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor even depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-251118">
		<div id="div-comment-251118" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-251118">
		January 9, 2015 at 5:20 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=251118#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-251118", "251118", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>For now, doPrint() has to be declared before the main function.  I&#8217;ll discuss how to get around this restriction in later sections.</p>
<p>doPrint() doesn&#8217;t have to be capitalized.  It could be called doprint() or do_print().  C++ is case-sensitive though, so doprint() and doPrint() would be considered two different functions.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/21b2deaaa0bcf172aa88051b6cf7ba1f?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://2.gravatar.com/avatar/21b2deaaa0bcf172aa88051b6cf7ba1f?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname"><a href='http://127.0.0.1/' rel='external nofollow' class='url'>zingmars</a></span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-102793">
		August 19, 2013 at 5:20 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=102793#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-102793", "102793", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to zingmars'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>How did you define the function doubleNumber? It was doubleNumber( int x ) {}, right?<br />
See, the int x part is the input part of the function - you list all the things that go into the function. You can&#8217;t actually specify specific variable names though, you can only specify the name that will be used in the function (can be anything you want, that&#8217;s how you&#8217;ll refer to the input inside the function), and the type (int, float, string, etc).<br />
So the reason it&#8217;s doubleNumber(x), is because you have to mention which variable to pass to doubleNumber. That way you can also call it like doubleNumber(y) and doubleNumber(z), and it will double the variables y and z.</p>
		</div>
</li><!-- #comment-## -->

        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=127796#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-127796", "127796", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to kenwhite23'> &middot; Reply</a></span>

		<p>Figured it out,  i needed to add the return x into function user() and then adjust main() to cout &lt;&lt; doublenumber(user(x)) &lt;&lt; endl;</p>
<p>Here is the working version!<br />
#include<br />
#include<br />
using namespace std;</p>
<p>int user(int x)<br />
{<br />
cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Pick a number to double: &quot; &lt;&gt; x;<br />
return x;<br />
}</p>
<p>int doublenumber(int x)<br />
{<br />
return 2 * x;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
int x;<br />
cout &lt;&lt; doublenumber(user(x)) &lt;&lt; endl;<br />
system(&quot;pause&quot;); //can also be replaced with getchar() to make portable<br />
return 0;<br />
}</p>
		</div>

<p>#include &quot;stdafx.h&quot;<br />
#include &lt;iostream&gt;</p>
<p>int SetIntN()<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;using std::cin;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;int n = 0;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cin &gt;&gt; n;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return n;<br />
}</p>
<p>int add(int x, int y)<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return x + y;<br />
}</p>
<p>int multiply(int Na, int Nb)<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;return Na * Nb;<br />
}</p>
<p>int main()<br />
{<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;using std::cout;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;choose x: &quot;;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;int x = SetIntN();<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;you chose x: &quot; &lt;&lt; x &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; std::endl;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;choose y: &quot;;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;int y = SetIntN();<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;you chose y: &quot; &lt;&lt; y &lt;&lt; &quot; &quot; &lt;&lt; std::endl;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Constant Z multiplies your answer by 3&quot; &lt;&lt; std::endl;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; &quot;your final answer is: &quot; &lt;&lt; x &lt;&lt; &quot; + &quot; &lt;&lt; y &lt;&lt; &quot; * &quot; &lt;&lt; 3 &lt;&lt; std::endl;<br />
&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;&nbsp;cout &lt;&lt; multiply(add(x, y), 3) &lt;&lt; std::endl;</p>

}</p>

		<p>Hello Alex,</p>
<p>Thank you very much for these fantastic tutorials, I am slowly working my way through them and have a question in regards to the functions for simple things such as addition, division,multiply.</p>
<p>At least in the context of these tutorials, why can one not simply put&#8230;</p>
<p>cout &lt;&lt; x * 2;</p>
<p>cout &lt;&lt; x + y;</p>
<p>I have tried the above (after assigning a value to x and y of-course), I still get the same result as I would have done when using a function.</p>
<p>Are there examples where the method above would be favorable over the creation of functions?</p>
<p>I can sort of see where a function would be useful in describing/showing why something is being done, but this could easily be done with comments.</p>
<p>At first I thought it would be for reusing the function with different variables for example in a game where you want to double several scores or items, but wouldn&apos;t you then have to include the variables within the function so that it gets returned what it expects?</p>
<p>It just seems like extra work.</p>
<p>I hope i haven&apos;t shown a huge misunderstanding of programming by asking this question. I just want to be 100% in the understanding of why I am doing things a certain way.</p>
<p>Kind regards</p>
<p>Daniel</p>
		</div>

{<br />

		<li class="comment even thread-odd thread-alt depth-1 clearfix" id="comment-252308">
		<div id="div-comment-252308" class="clearfix comment-container">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/d209081a7d3d5e5d47581c68657e0b42?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/d209081a7d3d5e5d47581c68657e0b42?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Nagidal</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-252308">
		May 2, 2015 at 5:05 am</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=252308#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252308", "252308", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Nagidal'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>I think that the function doubleNumber cannot be of int type. It has to be able to take *any* integer and return its double value. That includes also large (signed) integers like 2147483647 (31 bit long integer). Hence doubleNumber must be of long (or even long long) type:</p>
<p></p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

		<div id="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851" class="crayon-syntax crayon-theme-familiar-learncppcom crayon-font-monaco crayon-os-pc print-yes notranslate" data-settings=" no-popup minimize scroll-mouseover wrap" style=" margin-top: 12px; margin-bottom: 12px; font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;">
		
			<div class="crayon-plain-wrap"></div>
			<div class="crayon-main" style="">
				<table class="crayon-table">
					<tr class="crayon-row">
				<td class="crayon-nums " data-settings="show">
					<div class="crayon-nums-content" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important;"><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-1">1</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-2">2</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-3">3</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-4">4</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-5">5</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-6">6</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-7">7</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-8">8</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-9">9</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-10">10</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-11">11</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-12">12</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-13">13</div><div class="crayon-num crayon-striped-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-14">14</div><div class="crayon-num" data-line="crayon-555d747ed1f22223472851-15">15</div></div>
				</td>
						<td class="crayon-code"><div class="crayon-pre" style="font-size: 12px !important; line-height: 15px !important; -moz-tab-size:4; -o-tab-size:4; -webkit-tab-size:4; tab-size:4;"><div         <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=252312#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252312", "252312", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>The implementation of doubleNumber() above is flawed, as you correctly point out.</p>
<p>For now, that&#8217;s okay.  We&#8217;re more focused on function basics (parameters and return values) than on other data types (such as long long), overflow, and conversion between types.  We&#8217;ll get to all of those things later.</p>
<p>At this point in the tutorials, simplicity wins the day. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
		</div>

x = doubleNumber(x);</p>

Please help.<br />

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-2 clearfix" id="comment-252505">
		<div id="div-comment-252505" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">

		May 18, 2015 at 3:51 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=252505#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252505", "252505", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>x = 2x does makes sense if you think about it like this: x = (2x).  Whatever value is in x, double it, and then assign that value back to x.</p>
<p>So if x starts as 5, we double it to 10, and assign it back to x.  x now equals 10.</p>
<p>The function call is doing exactly that.</p>
		</div>
<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment even depth-3 clearfix" id="comment-252514">

But I still find it tricky as we are doing two things at the same time in</p><!-- Crayon Syntax Highlighter v2.7.1 -->

				<table class="crayon-table">

<p>It would work if the program first evaluates doublenumber() and then equates it to x.<br />
It won&#8217;t if it does the other way round.<br />
How does the program know what to do first.<br />
Or does it do both at the same time?<br />
Am I over thinking it?<br />
Thanks!</p>

<ul class="children">
		<li class="comment byuser comment-author-alex bypostauthor odd alt depth-4 clearfix" id="comment-252515">
		<div id="div-comment-252515" class="clearfix comment-container bypostauthor">
		<div class="comment-author vcard">
		<img alt='' src='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=65&#038;d=mm&#038;r=g' srcset='http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/a4e62d28c29f6c864923207d4c17993b?s=130&amp;d=mm&amp;r=g 2x' class='avatar avatar-65 photo' height='65' width='65' />		<span class="authorname">Alex</span>
		</div>
				<div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
		<a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/#comment-252515">
		May 18, 2015 at 8:42 pm</a>
        <span class="comment-reply-link"><a class='comment-reply-link' href='/cpp-tutorial/1-4a-a-first-look-at-function-parameters/?replytocom=252515#respond' onclick='return addComment.moveForm( "comment-252515", "252515", "respond", "972" )' aria-label='Reply to Alex'> &middot; Reply</a></span>		 
		</div>
		<p>You aren&#8217;t overthinking it.  On the contrary, you&#8217;re thinking ahead. <img src="http://www.learncpp.com/blog/wp-includes/images/smilies/simple-smile.png" alt=":)" class="wp-smiley" style="height: 1em; max-height: 1em;" /></p>
<p>C++ has a set of rules that it uses to evaluates all expressions.  How this works is covered in section <a href="http://www.learncpp.com/cpp-tutorial/31-precedence-and-associativity/" rel="nofollow">3.1 -- precedence and associativity</a>.</p>
<p>In this case, the evaluation of doublenumber(x) always happens before the assignment, because evaluation of functions is higher precedence than assignment.</p>
		</div>

				jQuery('body').on('click', 'a.share-pinterest', function(e){
					e.preventDefault();

					// Load Pinterest Bookmarklet code
					var s = document.createElement("script");
					s.type = "text/javascript";
					s.src = window.location.protocol + "//assets.pinterest.com/js/pinmarklet.js?r=" + ( Math.random() * 99999999 );
					var x = document.getElementsByTagName("script")[0];
					x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);

					var s = document.createElement("script");

					s.src = this + ( this.toString().indexOf( '?' ) ? '&' : '?' ) + 'js_only=1';
					var x = document.getElementsByTagName("script")[0];
					x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
